- **Date:** 2004-10-24T03:15:27+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<up4mn0dd1h3d8o92vqkaimf59dblcpuvsa@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<1ngfn0ddj297ctd0it16f3abip2dojtqfe@4ax.com> <217e491a.0410212141.576d6cc3@posting.google.com> <im6in05frv6o0k6jiedsatjall65e0b86p@4ax.com> <eOaed.58437$JG5.980778@news20.bellglobal.com> <0epin0p8155dsc5eqao2d9s49fhr8cvlqt@4ax.com> <NFfed.3161893$ic1.325550@news.easynews.com> <t78jn0ljbdpon6kqao42td963riaf3v8rc@4ax.com> <3Zled.3175587$ic1.326382@news.easynews.com> <c7kkn09vrs3uli27n81acsdgmmqk64jc6b@4ax.com> <AWCed.3812446$6p.636571@news.easynews.com>`

```
On Sun, 24 Oct 2004 00:45:20 GMT, "William M. Klein"
<wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>From:
>  http://www.iso.org/iso/en/CatalogueDetailPage.CatalogueDetail?CSNUMBER=4777&ICS1=35&ICS2=40&ICS3=
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>times you try this "trick".)

My original statement was:

"The Cobol standard does not specify file formats. They are
implementer-defined, an euphemism for non-standard. The only standard
we have is ASCII (despite Micro Focus' attempt to screw it up by
inserting nulls). Thus, line sequential files are standardized whereas
ANS85 sequential are non-standard. "

I have not back-pedaled from that.

>As I stated originally - (and you have yet to admit your error) there is no 
>standard for "text files" or "line sequential files" - they are no more 
>"Standard defined" then COBOL sequential files (which are "implementor defined 
>for format") - but are (in fact) LESS Standard (i.e. there is no standard 
>definition of them)

There is a standard definition of ASCII text files -- a blend of ISO
character set definitions plus de facto standards for file format.
Millions of these files are exchanged daily, and have been for 30
years.

The format of Cobol sequential files is completely implementor
defined, and lacks industry pressure or tradition for compatibility.
This week we saw an example of Cobol Set for AIX causing
incompatibility with sort running on the same platform. A much more
common difficulty experienced by outsiders who receive 'Cobol files'
is dealing with packed and binary fields, and converting to ASCII text
data between them. No ISO standard defines the format of binary or
packed. 

ASCII text files eliminate all those problems.

I thought one goal of Standardization was to make interchange easier.
That users had to create de facto standards says the Process isn't
working, isn't serving its stated goals.
```

```
So now you are saying that "line sequential" files are a DE FACTO standard - 
which include some (but not all) of the characters of ISO-646 (and maybe ISO 
10646)?    Is that your current claim?

(Of course ISO-646 includes several "control characters" and ISO 10646 includes 
MANY more).

*FILE* rather than "data" interchange is simply NOT something for which there is 
a current Standard.  (Actually, there may still be a Standard for "standard 
label tapes" - but I am not certain that such a Standard is still current).

The use of "line sequential" files in an IBM mainframe environment (and I 
suspect - but cannot personally vouch for - in most mainframe environments) is a 
MINISCULE portion of "sequential" files.  When "COBOL Standard sequential" files 
(which ARE very common in most mainframe environments) are "shared" across 
platforms, then there ARE conversion tools to transform them to the OPERATING 
SYSTEM SPECIFIC (not Standard, not portable) format of the receiving 
environment.

Personally, I do see that XML seems to be "growing" in popularity as a 
"transport" data format.  However, even there there is no (that I am aware of) 
"standard file format".  Certainly in IBM mainframe environments, they often 
come in (go out) in such things a CICS queues.

As Standard COBOL supports both CODE-SET and "record sequential" formats, I 
think that is the best existing (and source code portable) way to create files 
for cross-platform use.  I (personally) know of no "conforming" COBOL that 
supports these facilities that doesn't produce a file format that can be "read" 
by appropriate tools for use in that and other platforms.  This *MAY* require a 
conversion "tool" - but at least the program is portable and such tools are 
common.
```

On Sun, 24 Oct 2004 03:54:53 GMT, "William M. Klein"
<wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>So now you are saying that "line sequential" files are a DE FACTO standard -

My claim has not changed.

>(Of course ISO-646 includes several "control characters" and ISO 10646 includes 
>MANY more).

Control characters are a red herring. They are not used for data
exchange via text files. 

>*FILE* rather than "data" interchange is simply NOT something for which there is 
>a current Standard.  (Actually, there may still be a Standard for "standard 
>label tapes" - but I am not certain that such a Standard is still current).

"File" is ambiguous. If it means physical disks, of course the
directory format is defined by the file system. If it means
transmitted via network, the exchange protocol is defined by the
transfer program. 

There may not be an ISO standard for interchange but there are plenty
of industry-specific standards, including international ones. For
example, EDI and SWIFT.

>The use of "line sequential" files in an IBM mainframe environment (and I 
>suspect - but cannot personally vouch for - in most mainframe environments) is a 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>environment.

Line sequential requires no conversion tool except simple conversions
done by the transfer tool. That's why it is so widely used on
non-mainframe platforms, especially by companies that exchange large
numbers of files daily e.g. data warehouse.

>Personally, I do see that XML seems to be "growing" in popularity as a 
>"transport" data format.  However, even there there is no (that I am aware of) 
>"standard file format".  Certainly in IBM mainframe environments, they often 
>come in (go out) in such things a CICS queues.

When XML is transferred between non-mainframes, it is packaged as
ASCII text i.e. terminated by Cr and/or Lf. That applies to online
transactions as well as records coming from an actual text file.

>As Standard COBOL supports both CODE-SET and "record sequential" formats, I 
>think that is the best existing (and source code portable) way to create files 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>common.

I gave reasons why I disagree -- binary, packed, non-standard formats
of both. You have provided no substantiation for your preference.

We have seen here examples of frustrated users who received such files
and didn't know how to read them. I have never heard of such problems
with text files.

.    On  24.10.04
  wrote  robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests (Robert Wagner)
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
     in  bntmn01ba71iuv5aiu6fltvo8pia277jc8@4ax.com

RW> We have seen here examples of frustrated users who received such
RW> files and didn't know how to read them. I have never heard of such
RW> problems with text files.

   Maybe your ears are shut or tuned in a different direction.

   There are LOTS of problems with textfiles, even when that means  
simple text with line endings. It starts with which line-ending  
convention is used: is it Mac, PC, or Unix?

   Next comes the character set - is it IA5-IRV? Any national  
reference version? Which one? Some IBM Code Page? Which one? Microsoft  
Code Page? Maybe EBCDIC? ISO 8859, but which part? ISO 6937? KO-IR? Is  
it ISO 2022 encoded? UCS or Unicode? Then which encoding? UTF-7, UTF- 
8, or UTF-16? Big-Endian or Little-Endian?

   All this has to be known by the user and/or his application; for a  
simple text FILE as transmitted on a floppy or other recording medium,  
all this information is _NOT_ provided by the file system.

   In data communication, the situation is somewhat better since a  
number of protocols (not e.g. FTP) provide information about the  
character set, but also the register of character sets is not, ahem,  
perfect.

   But for all files on a recording medium, and those tranferred by  
e.g. FTP, there needs to be a bilateral agreement between the two  
partners, or a common convention (sometimes a dictate) for a given  
application, like the electronic banking which I mentioned in an  
earlier post.

   BTW, what I see as data being transferred, I see more Excel files  
or Winword files than simple textfiles. Not satisfactory, either...


   A problem with all this is that the Unix and PC systems do not have  
a file system which provides a record structure to applications, but a  
stream structure, where the file is an unstructured stream of bytes,  
and it is left completely to the application how to use it and how to  
build some cyclical structure in it.


  Mainframe systems as I know them have a file system where files are  
structured in records, and any application can call on the OS "give me  
the next record" without having to know beforehand how long that  
record is.

  BTW, I don't think that ORGANISATION LINE SEQUENTIAL is a good  
solution; COBOL provided always a way to specify variable length  
records and a "RECORD DELIMITER IS <clause>" which should have been  
used to specify text files with whatever character or character  
combination as delimiter.

   Coming back to the original question: many of your ideas are right,  
but you package it in a theory which contains many errors.

On 24 Oct 2004 14:31:00 GMT, l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms)
wrote:

>.    On  24.10.04

…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>convention is used: is it Mac, PC, or Unix?

It's easy to write software that works with all three. For instance,
my text editor does. 

>   Next comes the character set - is it IA5-IRV? Any national  
>reference version? Which one? Some IBM Code Page? Which one? Microsoft  
>Code Page? Maybe EBCDIC? ISO 8859, but which part? ISO 6937? KO-IR? Is  
>it ISO 2022 encoded? UCS or Unicode? Then which encoding? UTF-7, UTF- 
>8, or UTF-16?

Diacritical marks and non-Latin alphabets are not a big concern in the
US. We assume 10646-UTF-1 unless another is specified.

> Big-Endian or Little-Endian?

There are no binary numbers in text files. 

>   All this has to be known by the user and/or his application; for a  
>simple text FILE as transmitted on a floppy or other recording medium,  
>all this information is _NOT_ provided by the file system.

The above defaults handle 99% of the files I've seen .. thousands per
day in high-volume shops.

>   But for all files on a recording medium, and those tranferred by  
>e.g. FTP, there needs to be a bilateral agreement between the two  
>partners, or a common convention (sometimes a dictate) for a given  
>application, like the electronic banking which I mentioned in an  
>earlier post.

For general-purpose file transfer in the US, the common convention is
10646-UTF-1 ASCII text. If other, which occurs seldom, the burden is
on the sender to specify.

>   BTW, what I see as data being transferred, I see more Excel files  
>or Winword files than simple textfiles. Not satisfactory, either...

Sounds like your audience is end-users who transfer files
infrequently. In my experience, even those people usually save Excel
as csv for transport.

>   A problem with all this is that the Unix and PC systems do not have  
>a file system which provides a record structure to applications, but a  
>stream structure, where the file is an unstructured stream of bytes,  
>and it is left completely to the application how to use it and how to  
>build some cyclical structure in it.

Unix and PC have a layer that provides the same functionality --
stdio. The problem with mainframes is their Promethian notion that the
operating system is the provenance of intra-file management. By
failing to allow a mountable file system, they stifle progress. For
instance, SMB and NFS were developed by third-parties and mounted on
Windows and Unix without modifying the operating system. Mainframers
first tried to do it with clunky methods like IND$FILE and RJE, and
bypassing the operating system with SAN switches. When they got around
to doing it right, they had to wait for IBM to change zFS, which
didn't happen until 2002 .. six years late.

>  Mainframe systems as I know them have a file system where files are  
>structured in records, and any application can call on the OS "give me  
>the next record" without having to know beforehand how long that  
>record is.

Fgets() does the same.

>  BTW, I don't think that ORGANISATION LINE SEQUENTIAL is a good  
>solution; COBOL provided always a way to specify variable length  
>records and a "RECORD DELIMITER IS <clause>" which should have been  
>used to specify text files with whatever character or character  
>combination as delimiter.

That solution is specific to Cobol. When interchanging files, we
cannot assume the other party has a Cobol compiler or wants to write
programs to import data. We need a solution that's independent of
platform and compiler.

```
.    On  24.10.04
  wrote  robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests (Robert Wagner)
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
     in  6vqnn0lmndfuipd4t4dphvh6kd8vqgec3i@4ax.com
  about  Re: Report enhancements


RW>>> We have seen here examples of frustrated users who received such
RW>>> files and didn't know how to read them. I have never heard of such
RW>>> problems with text files.

>>   All this has to be known by the user and/or his application; for
>>   a simple text FILE as transmitted on a floppy or other recording
>>   medium, all this information is _NOT_ provided by the file
>>   system.

RW> The above defaults handle 99% of the files I've seen .. thousands
RW> per day in high-volume shops.

   The "I've seen" is an important restriction.

   Again - I don't doubt that lots of textfiles with lines terminated  
by CF/LF are being exchanged, and that this works satisfactorily. But  
I took and take issue that you build a theory and generalizations  
around this fact which lead to errors in other fields.

   Let me again take up some of those:

>>   There are LOTS of problems with textfiles, even when that means
>> simple text with line endings. It starts with which line-ending
>> convention is used: is it Mac, PC, or Unix?

RW> It's easy to write software that works with all three. For instance,
RW> my text editor does.

   Sure, this additional complication can be coped with. But for a  
mass data exchange, it would better to have a single format.


>>   Next comes the character set - is it IA5-IRV? Any national
>> reference version? Which one? Some IBM Code Page? Which one? Microsoft
>> Code Page? Maybe EBCDIC? ISO 8859, but which part? ISO 6937? KO-IR? Is
>> it ISO 2022 encoded? UCS or Unicode? Then which encoding? UTF-7, UTF-
>> 8, or UTF-16?

RW> Diacritical marks and non-Latin alphabets are not a big concern in
RW> the US.

  Well, how lightily you dismiss writing people's names correctly ...  
and look narrowly at the USA alone. There is a world beyond the USA's  
borders! Really!

RW> We assume 10646-UTF-1 unless another is specified.

  What does that mean?

>> Big-Endian or Little-Endian?

RW> There are no binary numbers in text files.

   Didn't you just mention "10646-UTF-1"? Whatever that means, I spoke  
of UTF-16, and allow me to quote from RFC 2781 by P. Hoffman and F.  
Yergeau, dated February 2000:

--------- schnipp -----------------------------------------
   In ISO 10646, each character is assigned a number, which Unicode
   calls the Unicode scalar value. This number is the same as the
   UCS-4 value of the character, and this document will refer to it
   as the "character value" for brevity. In the UTF-16 encoding,
   characters are represented using either one or two unsigned 16-bit
   integers, depending on the character value.

nd

   The rules for how characters are encoded in UTF-16 are:

   -  Characters with values less than 0x10000 are represented as a
      single 16-bit integer with a value equal to that of the character
      number.

   -  Characters with values between 0x10000 and 0x10FFFF are
      represented by a 16-bit integer with a value between 0xD800 and
      0xDBFF (within the so-called high-half zone or high surrogate
      area) followed by a 16-bit integer with a value between 0xDC00 and
      0xDFFF (within the so-called low-half zone or low surrogate area).

   -  Characters with values greater than 0x10FFFF cannot be encoded in
      UTF-16.



and further:

   Any labelling application that uses UTF-16 character encoding, and
   explicitly labels the text, and knows the serialization order of the
   characters in text, SHOULD label the text as either "UTF-16BE" or
   "UTF-16LE", whichever is appropriate based on the endianness of the
   text. This allows applications processing the text, but unable to
   look inside the text, to know the serialization definitively.

   Text in the "UTF-16BE" charset MUST be serialized with the octets
   which make up a single 16-bit UTF-16 value in big-endian order.
   Systems labelling UTF-16BE text MUST NOT prepend a BOM to the text.

   Text in the "UTF-16LE" charset MUST be serialized with the octets
   which make up a single 16-bit UTF-16 value in little-endian order.
   Systems labelling UTF-16LE text MUST NOT prepend a BOM to the text.

   Any labelling application that uses UTF-16 character encoding, and
   puts an explicit charset label on the text, and does not know the
   serialization order of the characters in text, MUST label the text as
   "UTF-16", and SHOULD make sure the text starts with 0xFEFF.

------------------ schnapp --------------------------------

   So far for "no binary numbers in text files"

>>   But for all files on a recording medium, and those tranferred by
>> e.g. FTP, there needs to be a bilateral agreement between the two
>> partners, or a common convention (sometimes a dictate) for a given
>> application, like the electronic banking which I mentioned in an
>> earlier post.

RW> For general-purpose file transfer in the US, the common convention is
RW> 10646-UTF-1 ASCII text.

   When "10646" is to mean ISO 10464, then it can't be ASCII, one of  
the national variants of ISO 646. ISO 10646 is a 32-bit-character set,  
and ISO 646 a 7-bit character set.

RW> If other, which occurs seldom, the burden is on the sender to
RW> specify.

   Which makes the "standard" less useful. Better there is a bilateral  
agreement on what is possible, allowed, and necessary.


>>   BTW, what I see as data being transferred, I see more Excel files
>> or Winword files than simple textfiles. Not satisfactory, either...

RW> Sounds like your audience is end-users who transfer files
RW> infrequently. In my experience, even those people usually save Excel
RW> as csv for transport.

   Experiences differ. I do recommend sending RTF and CSV files  
instead of Microsoft-binaries, but most people don't listen to me...


>>   A problem with all this is that the Unix and PC systems do not have
>> a file system which provides a record structure to applications, but a
>> stream structure, where the file is an unstructured stream of bytes,
>> and it is left completely to the application how to use it and how to
>> build some cyclical structure in it.

RW> Unix and PC have a layer that provides the same functionality --
RW> stdio.

>>  Mainframe systems as I know them have a file system where files are
>> structured in records, and any application can call on the OS "give me
>> the next record" without having to know beforehand how long that
>> record is.

RW> Fgets() does the same.

   No, it doesn't. Unix and DOS file systems don't know anything about  
records. On those systems, a record is something defined by an  
application, not by the system. For the system, any file is just a  
stream of bytes, and each application has to have prior knowledge on  
how to find some structure in it.

   On the OS/1100 mainframe, which I happen to know, a file is rather  
a linked list of records which each has a definite length (the length  
being specified in the record itself), while Unix and DOS only knows  
about a more or less infinite stream of bytes. I think that other  
mainframe operating systems, like IBM's, BS2000 (Fujitsu-Siemens'  
version of TSOS), Unisys MCP (are there any others left?) are very  
similar to this record concept.

   fgets() does not provide a record, but the number of bytes  
requested, see the following from the ISO-Standard on C:

--------- schnipp -----------------------------------------

   char *fgets(char * restrict s, int n, FILE * restrict stream);

   The _fgets_ function reads at most one less than the number of  
characters specified by _n_ from the stream pointed to by _stream_  
into the array pointed to by _s._ No additional characters are read  
after a new-line character (which is retained) or after end-of-file. A  
null character is written immediately after the last character read  
into the array.

------------------ schnapp --------------------------------

   So the application has to check the last character before the first  
null, if it is a line-terminator, and can only determine by this if  
the text line has been read in completely. The function will only  
return _n_ bytes , and not more, in case the text line may have been  
longer. And it has to scan the file contents to find the line- 
terminator. The mainframe record-based file system which I mentioned  
"knows" from the length indicator in the record control word, how many  
words (or bytes) are in the record and have to be returned to the  
requesting application. No need to exclude binary values or control  
characters.

   I do not want to enter in a discussion about relative merits or  
superiority, but only point to the fact that it is _different_ and not  
"the same".


>>  BTW, I don't think that ORGANISATION LINE SEQUENTIAL is a good
>> solution; COBOL provided always a way to specify variable length
>> records and a "RECORD DELIMITER IS <clause>" which should have been
>> used to specify text files with whatever character or character
>> combination as delimiter.

RW> That solution is specific to Cobol. When interchanging files, we
RW> cannot assume the other party has a Cobol compiler or wants to write
RW> programs to import data. We need a solution that's independent of
RW> platform and compiler.

   No, using the clause "RECORD DELIMITER IS <whatever>" would only be  
the COBOL way to specify that the record is delimited by a LF, a CR,  
or a LF/CR pair, or even an ASCII RS = Record Separator. I only say  
that this would be a more standard form of specifying this instead of  
introducing a new file ORGANISATION namely LINE SEQUENTIAL.


Yours,
Lï¿½ko Willms                                     http://www.willms-edv.de
/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --

Der Amerikaner, der den Kolumbus zuerst entdeckte, machte eine bï¿½se Entdeckung. -G.C.Lichtenberg

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Report enhancements

_(reply depth: 27)_

- **From:** Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests>
- **Date:** 2004-10-25T18:59:34+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0neqn0d5g1b8cp44l5koqi1oe117hru29f@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<im6in05frv6o0k6jiedsatjall65e0b86p@4ax.com> <9JTdgSxeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <6vqnn0lmndfuipd4t4dphvh6kd8vqgec3i@4ax.com> <9JXmHzAPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
On 25 Oct 2004 15:44:00 GMT, l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms)
wrote:

>.    On  24.10.04
>  wrote  robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests (Robert Wagner)
>     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
>     in  6vqnn0lmndfuipd4t4dphvh6kd8vqgec3i@4ax.com
>  about  Re: Report enhancements

>   Again - I don't doubt that lots of textfiles with lines terminated  
>by CF/LF are being exchanged, and that this works satisfactorily. But  
>I took and take issue that you build a theory and generalizations  
>around this fact which lead to errors in other fields.

Generalizations are bad netiquette in this newsgroup, so I limited
statements to my humble observations.

>   Let me again take up some of those:
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>mass data exchange, it would better to have a single format.

If we were inventing the civilized world, people would speak a single
language. As a practical matter, we cannot undo history. 

>>>   Next comes the character set - is it IA5-IRV? Any national
>>> reference version? Which one? Some IBM Code Page? Which one? Microsoft
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>borders! Really!

I'm saying 7-bit 626 is adequate for English, and the other code
standards (ex EBCDIC) are compatible with it for numbers, letters and
basic punctuation. For extended alphabets, I'm happy to follow
standards developed elsewhere.

>RW> We assume 10646-UTF-1 unless another is specified.
>
>  What does that mean?

It means Unicode version UTF-8.

>>> Big-Endian or Little-Endian?
>
>RW> There are no binary numbers in text files.
>
>   Didn't you just mention "10646-UTF-1"? Whatever that means, 

The standard requires big-endian:

"UCS characters U+0000 to U+007F (ASCII) are encoded simply as bytes
0x00 to 0x7F (ASCII compatibility). This means that files and strings
which contain only 7-bit ASCII characters have the same encoding under
both ASCII and UTF-8. 
All UCS characters >U+007F are encoded as a sequence of several bytes,
each of which has the most significant bit set. Therefore, no ASCII
byte (0x00-0x7F) can appear as part of any other character. 
The first byte of a multibyte sequence that represents a non-ASCII
character is always in the range 0xC0 to 0xFD and it indicates how
many bytes follow for this character. All further bytes in a multibyte
sequence are in the range 0x80 to 0xBF. This allows easy
resynchronization and makes the encoding stateless and robust against
missing bytes. 
All possible 231 UCS codes can be encoded. 
UTF-8 encoded characters may theoretically be up to six bytes long,
however 16-bit BMP characters are only up to three bytes long. 
The sorting order of Bigendian UCS-4 byte strings is preserved. 
The bytes 0xFE and 0xFF are never used in the UTF-8 encoding. "
http://www.cl.cam.ac.uk/~mgk25/unicode.html#utf-8


>RW> For general-purpose file transfer in the US, the common convention is
>RW> 10646-UTF-1 ASCII text.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>and ISO 646 a 7-bit character set.

For characters <128, they are the same.

>   fgets() does not provide a record, but the number of bytes  
>requested, ...
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>"the same".

Functionally it is the same -- it reads the next record. Technical
details are different. As you say, the program must execute a command
to 'measure' the record length, if it needs to know.  

>>>  BTW, I don't think that ORGANISATION LINE SEQUENTIAL is a good
>>> solution; COBOL provided always a way to specify variable length
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>introducing a new file ORGANISATION namely LINE SEQUENTIAL.

I want to say RECORD DELIMITER IS PLATFORM-STANDARD in order to be
portable. If I'm migrating from mainframe to Unix, I don't want to
change all the NLs to LFs. LINE SEQUENTIAL gives me that
platform-independence.
```

```
Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote

> >   fgets() does not provide a record, but the number of bytes  
> >requested, ...
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Functionally it is the same -- it reads the next record. 

No. Wrong. Read what was written.  It reads up to the next newline
_OR_ until the buffer length - 1.  It is up to the program to check
whether this is the complete line or only part of it by checking for
the newline.

Whether the complete 'line' is a 'record' or is just some arbitrary
gathering of bytes which may be part of a 'record', or several
'records', or nothing useful at all is entirely up to the application.
```

l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms) wrote in message news:<9JTdgSxeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>...

message snipped

Perhaps the use of XML with schemas or DTDs may help resolve some of
these problems, I don't claim to know very much about XML, but it does
seem to present a way forward for applications to specify how a file
and its records are organised so that a recipient is automatically
informed what convention is used.

I would still wish to use a vendor's own formats for files internal to
the application, because I still think that minimising file sizes and
execution times is important, though not to the point of using 2 digit
year numbers, etc.

The COBOL standards committee are developing a facility for handling
XML, but I also understand that there is nothing to stop people doing
it themselves with the existing standards, though perhaps with rather
more difficulty.

Robert
```

On 24 Oct 2004 12:49:53 -0700, rjones0@hotmail.com (Robert Jones)

Cobol-format files make a big deal over saving space with packed and
binary, then waste twice what they saved by shipping trailing spaces
and leading zeros.

Consider that a zero in comp-3 s9(9)v9(2) takes five bytes whereas
",," takes one byte for the field. 

Even more counteractive: saving size with comp-3, then squandering 50
bytes for-future-expansion.

```
Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote 

> >(Of course ISO-646 includes several "control characters" and ISO 10646 includes 
> >MANY more).
> 
> Control characters are a red herring. They are not used for data
> exchange via text files. 

One of the most formalised of structured data exchange standards,
EDIFAC, does not use CR/LF in any combination.  It defines its data in
segments which are terminated by segment terminators, usually
apostrophe.

It is primarily informal data exchange (ie application defined such as
CSV) that just uses 'text files' divided into 'lines'.
 
> There may not be an ISO standard for interchange but there are plenty
> of industry-specific standards, including international ones. For
> example, EDI and SWIFT.

EDI does not use line terminators of CR/LF, it happens that everything
between the formal segment terminator and the next segment (as
identified by a character > space) is ignored and need not exist.  In
some cases control characters (such as CR/LF) _must_ not exist.

> When XML is transferred between non-mainframes, it is packaged as
> ASCII text i.e. terminated by Cr and/or Lf. That applies to online
> transactions as well as records coming from an actual text file.

HTML and XML have no requirement at all for CR/LF to exist. 
Whitespace is completely ignored and can be stripped out.

Your assertion that 'it is ASCII text terminated by CR/LF' is wrong. 
It is ASCII text that may, or need not be, contaminated by various
control codes.

The presense of the CR/LF and TABs is purely for human consumption and
is nothing to do with data exchange.

> We have seen here examples of frustrated users who received such files
> and didn't know how to read them. I have never heard of such problems
> with text files.

Oh, you mean like C-ISAM data files with deleted records and junk
where records have not yet been written ?

Given that 'text files' may include EDIFAC, XML, or unnamed CSV data
then users may have no clue at all how to read and/or use the data.
```

```
Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote 

> Users don't care about standards; they care about portability. 

They care about neither.  They only want the data for the program they
want to use, they neither know nor care about 'standards' or what
issues are involved in portability.

This is why they send Excel spreadsheets and Word documents as EMail
attachments (with or without viruses).

If they actually cared about portability they would use a portable
format themselves.

> The
> standards process was created as a means to that end. If I have to
> choose between two formats, one more standardized and the other more
> portable, my preference is the latter. 

You seem to have gone off against an argument never made by anyone. 
What actual point do you imagine you are arguing against ?

> Nor have you. You have not shown that ASCII text is weaker nor defined
> by applications. In fact, it is defined by common practice and by
> operating systems. 

Nor have I even attempted to argue against ASCII or line terminated
files. I have been pointing out that:

   'LINE SEQUENTIAL' is not necessarily congruent to 'ASCII text
files'

   EDI/EDIFACT does not require nor care about cr/lf

   SEQUENTIAL is perfectly adequate for writing ASCII text (in many
cases),
   as is DISPLAY.  It does not _require_ LINE SEQUENTIAL.

   Many of your assertions are wrong, such as HTML and XML requiring
CR/LF.

You seem to have a fixation that 'ASCII text file' is inseparable from
and defined by 'LINE SEQUENTIAL' and vice versa.
 
> >> >Your assertion that 'it is ASCII text terminated by CR/LF' is wrong. 
> >> >It is ASCII text that may, or need not be, contaminated by various
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> it?

You are wrong that is _is_ terminated by CR/LF as a requirement.  It
may not be, or it may have them, but will completely ignore them (ie
they are contamination that are stripped out).

> >> >Given that 'text files' may include EDIFAC, XML, or unnamed CSV data
> >> >then users may have no clue at all how to read and/or use the data.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Make that 'communication format'. 

EDIFACT, XML, or other may, but need not, or in some cases must not,
have cr/lf control codes.  These are still 'text files'.

CSV and TSV formats certainly do require line terminators to indicate
to start on the next line (or row).

You proclaim to support standards but an extremely limited view of
what they are.

> >> Users are on my side.
> >
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> They also recognize transfers to and from Web sites. 

If you knew anything about web sites you should know that web page
content does not care about cr/lf at all (except in <pre> blocks).

in which case alpha fields will be padded with spaces and numeric
fields padded with leading zeros.

Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote 

> Excel's import wizard
> works surprizingly well on flat files. 

It may read in the data, but this doesn't mean that the data can be
understood, it is often just a series of numbers. This may mean
nothing to the user.

> I'm arguing against fixed-format fixed-length records (more
> standardized) and in favor of CSV ASCII text (more portable).

Yet Rick's was tab-separated, cr/lf terminated ASCII text.

The problem with CSV is that it is completely informal, the data is
just numbers and/or text with no structure and no inherent meaning.

EDIFACT is a formalised mechanism that identifies what each data item
means.  XML applications (not XML itself) formalise the data items as
well.
 
> Reality check. It is on IBM mainframe (except codeset is EBCDIC),
> Micro Focus, Fujitsu and Realia, at least. Can you name a compiler
> where LINE SEQUENTIAL is *not* ASCII text?

Yes, IBM Mainframe.  You just said it was EBCDIC, did you not notice ?
 There are also compilers where LINE SEQUENTIAL is not anything at
all.

> >   SEQUENTIAL is perfectly adequate for writing ASCII text (in many
> >cases),
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> written one byte at a time. 

NO.  WRONG. YOU CANNOT.  You certainly cannot on Fujitsu or
MicroFocus, I seriously doubt that you can on other systems.

> But it's not the most convenient way.

It is 'not convenient' because it simply doesn't work, unlike the
mechanisms that I suggested.

It seemed SEQUENTIAL was perfectly convenient in Rick's program, it is
perfectly convenient to use DISPLAY.

> People here like to recommend 'best tool for the job'. If LINE
> SEQUENTIAL is expressly designed for ASCII text, why do you resist
> using it? Is this a quasi-religious issue?

Where do you see me "resist using it" ?  I, however, have not argued,
as you have, that there is 'only one correct way' in a dogmatic way.

You may notice, if you actually read the messages, that my point has
always been that they can do it whatever way they wish as long as it
gets the results they require.

(Whereas RELATIVE/RANDOM does not get the right result at all).

> >You seem to have a fixation that 'ASCII text file' is inseparable from
> >and defined by 'LINE SEQUENTIAL' and vice versa.
> 
> It is.

I can have perfectly valid and useful ASCII text files that cannot be
read or written correctly as LINE SEQUENTIAL. There is a common subset
of 'ASCII text file' and 'LINE SEQUENTIAL' where there is an overlap,
that doesn't make the two congruent (except in your mind).

> I meant they think 'file transfer' is about downloading from a Web
> site. For once, they're right.

Most of the file transfer that I deal with is entirely automatic, _my_
users prefer it that way, they only notice it when they get an EMail
from my programs that tell them something failed, (their clients get
an EMail acknowlegement when it works).

If your users have to get involved with file transfer by downloading
from a web page then they may be poorly served.

On 26 Oct 2004 00:57:48 -0700, riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote 

>The problem with CSV is that it is completely informal, the data is
>just numbers and/or text with no structure and no inherent meaning.

Usually, but not necessarily. The first record can be a template
containing field names.

>EDIFACT is a formalised mechanism that identifies what each data item
>means.  XML applications (not XML itself) formalise the data items as
>well.

I don't dispute the superiority of EDIFACT and XML. They're not as
widely used or supported .. yet.

>It seemed SEQUENTIAL was perfectly convenient in Rick's program, it is
>perfectly convenient to use DISPLAY.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>Where do you see me "resist using it" ? 

In the paragraph above, where you advocate RECORD SEQUENTIAL and
DISPLAY, even though they do not conform to the OS standard. If such a
program was moved to another platform, code would have to be manually
converted. 

> I, however, have not argued,
>as you have, that there is 'only one correct way' in a dogmatic way.

I said ASCII text is the most widely used, not the only way.

>> >You seem to have a fixation that 'ASCII text file' is inseparable from
>> >and defined by 'LINE SEQUENTIAL' and vice versa.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>that doesn't make the two congruent (except in your mind).

The only definition of LINE SEQUENTIAL is in compiler manuals. The
Micro Focus version, below, says format follows the operating system
standard. In the case of Unix and Windows, that's variable length with
a terminator. In other words, ASCII text.

" the file is treated as consisting of fixed-length records, each
containing one line of data. The records are stored with trailing
spaces removed. The definition of a line of data varies among
different operating systems. Some terminate line records with one or
both of the carriage-return and line-feed characters, and some pad out
as fixed length records. Your COBOL system, therefore, always produces
files which are compatible in this respect with the editor software in
any operating system. "

>> I meant they think 'file transfer' is about downloading from a Web
>> site. For once, they're right.
>
>If your users have to get involved with file transfer by downloading
>from a web page then they may be poorly served.

They are not my (captive) users, they are the general public running
Web browsers.

On Sun, 24 Oct 2004 19:51:47 GMT, "William M. Klein"
<wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>
>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote in message 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>growing (and - in some "standards" - required)

ISO-10646 is a superset of ISO-646, indicated by their designation
similarity. As you pointed out, they standardize code set rather than
file format. 

I don't know whether XML defines file format. I'd guess it does not,
instead deferring to prior standards, which in this case are de facto.
That means records are still delimited by Cr and/or Lf.

Your point is well taken .. Unicode is the future code set standard.

- **References:** `<0epin0p8155dsc5eqao2d9s49fhr8cvlqt@4ax.com> <t78jn0ljbdpon6kqao42td963riaf3v8rc@4ax.com> <3Zled.3175587$ic1.326382@news.easynews.com> <c7kkn09vrs3uli27n81acsdgmmqk64jc6b@4ax.com> <AWCed.3812446$6p.636571@news.easynews.com> <up4mn0dd1h3d8o92vqkaimf59dblcpuvsa@4ax.com> <hIFed.3820586$6p.637682@news.easynews.com> <bntmn01ba71iuv5aiu6fltvo8pia277jc8@4ax.com> <nJTed.3267645$ic1.334258@news.easynews.com> <7vkon0tgomaj80u59int6kqpq298ok8sao@4ax.com>`

```
Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote 

> I don't know whether XML defines file format. I'd guess it does not,
> instead deferring to prior standards, which in this case are de facto.
> That means records are still delimited by Cr and/or Lf.

XML is not in 'records' at all.  It is irrelevant if there are control
codes or not, these are merely for human convenience where lf move to
the noxt line on the terminal or printer and cr returns to column 1
and tab moves to the next stop point.

The concept of a 'record' in XML is defined by the tags and takes no
accounts of how this is in display 'lines' or not.
```

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-10-25T12:18:30-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cljji7$rm7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<0epin0p8155dsc5eqao2d9s49fhr8cvlqt@4ax.com> <NFfed.3161893$ic1.325550@news.easynews.com> <t78jn0ljbdpon6kqao42td963riaf3v8rc@4ax.com> <3Zled.3175587$ic1.326382@news.easynews.com> <c7kkn09vrs3uli27n81acsdgmmqk64jc6b@4ax.com> <AWCed.3812446$6p.636571@news.easynews.com> <up4mn0dd1h3d8o92vqkaimf59dblcpuvsa@4ax.com> <hIFed.3820586$6p.637682@news.easynews.com> <bntmn01ba71iuv5aiu6fltvo8pia277jc8@4ax.com> <nJTed.3267645$ic1.334258@news.easynews.com> <7vkon0tgomaj80u59int6kqpq298ok8sao@4ax.com>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote in message
news:7vkon0tgomaj80u59int6kqpq298ok8sao@4ax.com...

> ISO-10646 is a superset of ISO-646, indicated by their designation
> similarity. As you pointed out, they standardize code set rather than
> file format.

Are you sure that's the appropriate conclusion to be drawn from the
designation similarity?

I observe that the rules about what sorts of signs are to be used to
identify shipboard ventilation systems (ISO 644) doesnt' strike me as being
an obvious predecessor to the specifications for the hardness classes of
washers in screw and washer assemblies (ISO 10644).  Nor do the
specification of information interchange formats for dates and times (ISO
8601) serve as a meaningful predecessor to the specifications and test
methods for micaceous iron oxides for paints (ISO 10601) or as a direct
successor to the specification of test parameters for friction and wear by
sliding for plastics (ISO 6601).

From these examples, and from my own experience, I believe the only
appropriate conclusion I could safely draw from the fact that both ISO 646
and ISO 10646 deal with character sets is that ISO issued numbers for some
ten thousand documents between them.

True enough, I don't have a copy of ISO/IEC 646 or ISO/IEC 10646 handy to
verify this, but I'd be curious to know if the latter ISO document (or any
other ISO document) supports the contention that the latter *number* was
assigned specifically to indicate its relationship to the former *number*.

I would be really, really surprised if either ISO 10646, or any other ISO
document for that matter, so stated, because I don't believe that's the way
the mechanisms for the assignment of ISO document numbers works.  The
similarity between the number assigned to ISO 646 and that assigned for ISO
10646 is, I am convinced barring evidence to the contrary, purely
happenstance.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

> 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> sequential file is "perfectly standard ANS85".

That is irrelevant to the issue.  Rick had used entirely standard
ANS85 Cobol syntax and produced an entirely standard text file that
was perfectly acceptable to whatever he wanted to feed it into.

Other systems may not be able to do that and thus may need to use
non-standard (ANS85) vendor extensions, if they have those available.
 
> The Cobol standard does not specify file formats. They are
> implementer-defined, an euphemism for non-standard. The only standard
> we have is ASCII (despite Micro Focus' attempt to screw it up by
> inserting nulls). Thus, line sequential files are standardized whereas
> ANS85 sequential are non-standard.

You are confused.  'Line sequential' is also just as
'implementor-defined' as every other file format.  Fujitsu defaults to
using specified record lengths, MF defaults to null insertion, others
may not have it at all.  Terminators may be combinations of CR and/or
LF.

If Rick reliably gets the output that _he_ requires then the program
works.

On 23 Oct 2004 11:39:15 -0700, riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote 

>> The Cobol standard does not specify file formats. They are
>> implementer-defined, an euphemism for non-standard. The only standard
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>'implementor-defined' as every other file format. 

No, it is not implementor-defined; it is defined by ISO-646 and
industry practice. 

> Fujitsu defaults to
>using specified record lengths, MF defaults to null insertion, others
>may not have it at all.  Terminators may be combinations of CR and/or
>LF.

When the compiler produces a non-standard file by default, we find a
way to make it deliver a standard one. In the case of MF, we turn off
null-insertion; in the case of Fujitsu, we supply the record length.

For a standards-friendly forum, I'm surprised at the amount of
resistance here to a standardized file format .. one that has been
very widely used for 30 years.

The rant is from your side, which seeks to shred all my postings
without regard to validity. I don't take offense. CLC volume increases
and thoughts are provoked. Rant on, let readers decide who's right.

>This is actually an advantage, programs can read the data by using the
>tabs as field separators, or, if they can't read it that way they can
>use the fixed field lengths and skip the tabs.

That's like saying punched cards were actually better. 

>> >All you seem to be objecting to is that it wasn't done _your_ way, a
>> >way that you seem to claim is the only 'correct' way.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>SEQUENTIAL, as you would have, to create an identical file.

If he'd made it a real tab-delimited file, leading zeros and trailing
spaces would have been truncated. 

>All your whining about 'standards' is completely bogus.  The file was
>completely within all you were winging about. It seems simply that you
>are trying to get Rick to use LINE SEQUENTIAL.

The message you are now reading was transported as LINE SEQUENTIAL. So
too were all your email messages. Why not write to the administrators
of those systems to say they're using a "completely bogus" format?
Tell them it's all my fault. They might thank you for the
enlightenment although it's far more likely they'll think you a kook. 

>I have great difficulty in believing that you were ever a manager of
>anything, the way that you argue and rant makes you appear to be a
>frustrated control freak.

Religion is Belief, which doesn't demand the rigor of argumentation.

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote in message
news:62mon0tg9bduajpohfjkvkeunkaqhcr1a1@4ax.com...
> On 24 Oct 2004 12:04:56 -0700, riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:
[snip]
> >Your rant started when Rick used the completely ANS85 Cobol SEQUENTIAL
> >and created a perfectly good file exactly as required.  Your only
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> spaces would have been truncated.

My point was to demonstrate how Report Writer may be
used to accomplish a particular task and to show that that
particular use could be widely adapted. The program was
simple. Compare that with the following, which I wrote
earlier today. This is not widely adaptable.

And to be clear, I ran this program to create the byte-stream
file, imported that file into a table in Access, exported from
Access the same table to a text file, and used WinDiff to
compare the files. They were identical.

-----
       identification division.
       program-id. tab-test.
       environment division.
       configuration section.
       special-names.
           symbolic characters
               separator-tab is 10
               line-feed is 11
               carriage-return is 14.
       input-output section.
       file-control.
           select item-file assign to "item.dat"
               organization is indexed
               access is sequential
               record key is item-id.
       data division.
       file section.
       fd item-file.
       01 item-record.
         05 item-id pic x(6).
         05 item-description pic x(30).
         05 item-price packed-decimal pic s9(5)v99.
         05 item-quantity comp pic 9(5).
       working-storage section.
       01 item-text-name pic x(9) value "item.txt" & x"00".
       01 access-mode pic x comp-x value 2.
       01 deny-mode pic x comp-x value 0.
       01 device pic x comp-x value 0.
       01 file-handle pic x(4).
       01 file-offset pic x(8) comp-x value 0.
       01 flags pic x comp-x value 0.
       01 byte-count pic x(4) comp-x value 0.
       01 string-buffer pic x(80).
       01 string-length comp-5 pic s9(4) value 0.
       01 work-id pic x(6).
       01 work-description pic x(30).
       01 work-price pic +9(5).99.
       01 work-quantity pic 9(5).
       01 text-length comp-5 pic s9(4) value 0.
       01 text-source comp-5 pic s9(4) value 0.
       01 number-work pic x(24).
       procedure division.
       begin.
           open input item-file
           call "CBL_CREATE_FILE" using item-text-name
               access-mode deny-mode device file-handle
           perform until exit
               read item-file
                   at end exit perform
               end-read
               move spaces to string-buffer
               perform create-string
               move string-length to byte-count
               call "CBL_WRITE_FILE" using file-handle
                   file-offset byte-count flags string-buffer
               add byte-count to file-offset
           end-perform
           call "CBL_CLOSE_FILE" using file-handle
           close item-file
           stop run.

       create-string.
           *> accepts string-buffer
           move 1 to string-length  *> used as position until end

           move function length (item-id) to text-length
           move item-id (1:text-length)
               to string-buffer (1:text-length)
           add text-length to string-length

           move separator-tab to string-buffer (string-length:1)
           add 1 to string-length

           perform get-description-length
           move item-description (1:text-length)
               to string-buffer (string-length:text-length)
           add text-length to string-length

           move separator-tab to string-buffer (string-length:1)
           add 1 to string-length

           move item-price to work-price
           move work-price (1:) to number-work
           perform compress-number
           move number-work (1:text-length)
               to string-buffer (string-length:text-length)
           add text-length to string-length

           move separator-tab to string-buffer (string-length:1)
           add 1 to string-length

           move item-quantity to work-quantity
           move work-quantity (1:) to number-work
           perform compress-number
           move number-work (1:text-length)
               to string-buffer (string-length:text-length)
           add text-length to string-length

           move carriage-return & line-feed
               to string-buffer (string-length:2)
           add 2 to string-length

           subtract 1 from string-length
           .  *> returns string-buffer string-length

       get-description-length.
           move function length (item-description)
               to text-length
           perform until text-length < 1
               if item-description (text-length:1) not = space
                   exit perform
               end-if
               subtract 1 from text-length
           end-perform
           .  *> returns text-length

       compress-number.
           *> accepts number-work
           *> uses text-length as position until end
           move 1 to text-length
           move 1 to text-source
           perform until exit
               evaluate number-work (text-source:1)
                 when "-"
                   add 1 to text-length text-source
                 when "+"
                 when "0"
                   add 1 to text-source
                 when "."  *> one "0" before decimal point
                   move "0" to number-work (text-length:1)
                   add 1 to text-length
                   exit perform
                 when other
                   exit perform
               end-evaluate
           end-perform
           if number-work (text-source:1) = space
                                   *> occurs with integer 0
               move "0" to number-work (text-length:1)
               add 1 to text-length text-source
           end-if
           perform until number-work (text-source:1) = space
               move number-work (text-source:1)
                   to number-work (text-length:1)
               add 1 to text-length text-source
           end-perform
           move spaces to number-work (text-length:)

           subtract 1 from text-length
           .  *> returns number-work text-length
       end program tab-test.
-----
```

Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote 

> The message you are now reading was transported as LINE SEQUENTIAL. 

Completely bogus.  These are _not_ 'LINE SEQUENTIAL' at all.  They
were not written by COBOL programs.  They _are_ ASCII text, but in
fact are HTML and the content need not have CR or LF codes at all,
line breaks are indicated by <br> or other means.

It may be that writing a file using a LINE SEQUENTIAL organization
may, but need not, result in a completely standard ASCII text file,
but so can SEQUENTIAL organization or DISPLAY on many systems.

entirely HTML.

It is your argument that is completely bogus, not the formats.

>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote in message
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>source code; not interoperability.

That's true for Cobol. I was referring to a broader scope such as ASC
X12. For instance, the EDI Standard said 11 years ago:

"This FIPS PUB recognizes the reality that some families of EDI
standards were developed to provide solutions to immediate needs, and
that inclusion of the goal of universality in their development would
have unacceptably delayed their availability. However, a future is
envisioned in which the benefits of universality outweigh the sunk
costs in specialized solutions, leading first to cooperation among
standards developers, then to harmonization of standards, and
eventually to a single universally accepted family of EDI standards. "
http://www.itl.nist.gov/fipspubs/fip161-2.htm

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote in message
news:7jvpn01ahr4cmrum6p9q252hp1cjh3r5hv@4ax.com...
> On Mon, 25 Oct 2004 00:31:39 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> >> >  Just admit that "line sequential" (like record sequential) is what
the
> >> >implementor WANTS it to be (in response to THEIR users - for THAT
> >environment).
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> That's true for Cobol. I was referring to a broader scope [...]

Mr Wagner, I have no doubt that a broader scope
is involved; but, perhaps, it is time for Hume's law
'that conclusions about what ought to be cannot be
deduced from premises stating only what was, what
is, or what will be--and the other way about.' It may
be that premises outside of 'what was, what is, and
what will be' are beyond one's immediate
understanding.

Mr Wagner, it appears to me that you have reached
a conclusion about what 'line sequential files' ought
to be and, used as premises, claims about 'what was,
what is, or what will be'. [I did not use 'only' because
I have not examined every exchange.] Hume's law
suggests that the conclusion is invalid.

Wouldn't it be wonderful if one could use
ORGANIZATION IS format-name to take care
of those nasty data interchange problems! Or, maybe
this was addressed, in COBOL 2002, by the FORMAT
clause, which I never really understood.

FDIS, page 285-287, 13.16.22 FORMAT clause,
"The FORMAT clause specifies that records written to
the file are to be formatted to an external representation
during the output process and records read from the file
are to be converted from the external format into internal
representation during the input process. The external
representation is suitable for presentation or printing."
...
GR10, "The implementor shall specify the representation
produced as a result of processing the FORMAT clause,
including any control information that may be necessary
for presentation or printing. This representation may be
the same as internal representation."

Could this mean tab-delimited, CSV, etc., as specified
by the implementor?
```

```
Robert Wagner <nospam-robert@wagner.net> wrote 

> I'd be embarrassed to send a file with trailing spaces and leading
> zeros. The receiver might ask 'Who wrote this thing, a Cobol
> programmer?'. 

If the data is correct and readable then I doubt that the user would
even notice. In fact if they did then the fixed sizes would make it
much more obvious how the data was arranged in columns.

Typically, CSV, and especially TSV, data when viewed as simple text
in, say, Notepad or using TYPE, is difficult to relate into columns,
they simply do not line up.

If viewed using a mechanism that recognised the field separators then
it would make no difference at all.

Thus the fixed field sizes make it much more flexible in how it is
viewed, make it more readily understandable by simple inspection, and
allow it to be imported in more different ways.

What _I_ would be embarressed about is if I made as many factual
errors, factoids, and misinformation as you seem quite happy to
publish.

For example about 90% of what you said about 64bit, use of RELATIVE
files, and dozens of others.


> But in answer to your question, the format is usable.

Thank you.

"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message
news:10nqc24l3tu30e2@corp.supernews.com...
>  ...             Or, maybe
> this was addressed, in COBOL 2002, by the FORMAT
> clause, which I never really understood.

No, that's not what the FORMAT clause is about.  FORMAT works with SELECT
WHEN, q.v.

The 2008 draft is expected to have variable-length elementary items that
includes the ability to specify that the length of an elementary item is
determined at execution time by a numeric prefix or by a trailing delimiter,
which is what I think is being asked for here.  See J4/04-0151 for the most
recent draft of this proposal.

    -Chuck Stevens

Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net.yourmammaharvests> wrote 

> >You are confused.  'Line sequential' is also just as
> >'implementor-defined' as every other file format. 
> 
> No, it is not implementor-defined; it is defined by ISO-646 and
> industry practice. 

LINE SEQUENTIAL is implementor defined in ANS85 (ie current
compilers). There is nothing in the standard that defines LINE
SEQUENTIAL as anything, let alone as ISO-646 and whatever 'industry
practice' you think applies.


> When the compiler produces a non-standard file by default, 

.. as defined by the implementor.

> we find a
> way to make it deliver a standard one. In the case of MF, we turn off
> null-insertion; in the case of Fujitsu, we supply the record length.
 
Or _we_ use other mechanisms to get the files that we require, such as
DISPLAY to stdout, or SEQUENTIAL files with CR/LF control codes.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Report enhancements

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2004-10-21T11:56:37-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0410211056.46f07981@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<41659C51.30908@optonline.net> <cl5r85$ate$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <10ndln2mem78r97@corp.supernews.com> <9JHJrpiuflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <10nf9km25av146a@corp.supernews.com>`

```
"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote

> .. and I changed the file organization to
> sequential, so that tabs will not have a leading nul character.

There is a run time switch 'N' which cotrols this. You could add '-N'
to the run command or to the COBSW= environment line, or change the
setting using the supplied CALL x"91" 13/14 routines.
```

On 20-Oct-2004, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:

> After loading some data into 'item.dat', I ran the following
> program to create 'item.lst'. I then loaded the list file into
…[47 more quoted lines elided]…

I just created some programs that downloaded data that were loaded into Oracle.
 My requirements included getting rid of extra spaces.

So after populating the output, line I needed to perform:

 8000-COMPRESS.
     PERFORM VARYING WORK-LINE-BRACE FROM WORK-LINE-size  BY -1
               UNTIL WORK-LINE-BRACE < 2
         IF WORK-LINE (WORK-LINE-BRACE:1) = SINGLE-BRACE
             PERFORM 8100-END-BRACE
         END-IF
     END-PERFORM.

 8100-END-BRACE.

     SUBTRACT 1              FROM WORK-LINE-BRACE
                           GIVING WORK-LINE-SPACE.
     MOVE WORK-LINE-BRACE      TO WORK-LINE-DEST.
     PERFORM VARYING WORK-LINE-SPACE FROM WORK-LINE-SPACE
                                         BY -1
               UNTIL WORK-LINE (WORK-LINE-SPACE:1) > SPACE
                  IF WORK-LINE (WORK-LINE-SPACE:1) = SPACE
                      MOVE WORK-LINE (WORK-LINE-DEST:)
                             TO WORK-LINE (WORK-LINE-SPACE:)
                      MOVE WORK-LINE-SPACE
                             TO WORK-LINE-DEST
                  END-IF
     END-PERFORM.

Data look like:
   03  TR-SEQUENCE          PIC 9(06)          VALUE ZERO.
   03  FILLER                       PIC X(03)          VALUE '{,{'.
   03  TR-LINE-DATE            PIC X(06)          VALUE SPACES.
   03  FILLER                       PIC X(03)          VALUE '{,{'.
   03  TR-LINE-SUBCODE     PIC X(05)          VALUE ZERO.
...
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Report enhancements

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms)
- **Date:** 2004-10-21T16:30:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9JHM$xQ9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<41659C51.30908@optonline.net> <9J4yzBgeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <cl5r85$ate$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
.    On  20.10.04
  wrote  howard@brazee.net (Howard Brazee)
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
     in  cl5r85$ate$1@peabody.colorado.edu
  about  Report enhancements


>>   COBOLs solution for this has been the REPORT WRITER, and for many
>> decades already. Unfortunately not implemented in every compiler.

HB> I don't think this is what we want.

   Maybe, but it is what I would offer, albeit with some changes...


HB> Even in the old days, REPORT WRITER made things about twice as easy
HB> to write reports as without it.   Twice as easy wasn't sufficient.

   I guess there was no way to do it even easier...


HB> But what we want (in Mainframe land) nowadays is to have a mainframe
HB> CoBOL program to create a report which the users can view on their PC
HB> and then decide to print on their printers set up the way they want,
HB> and in the format they want.    PC CoBOL has this same requirement -
HB> unless each computer has its separately compiled copy of the main
HB> program REPORT WRITER doesn't do enough.

   The solution is an HTML or XML document, or a CSV file.

   So why not specifying the type of output in the RD, i.e. REPORT  
DESCRIPTION? Or do that even dynamically?

   The idea came when I looked at the code examples from CobolScript  
(http://www.cobolscript.com), where HTML tags are embedded literally  
in the string being DISPLAYed, like this:

000097  PERFORM VARYING counter FROM 1 BY 1 UNTIL counter > 8
000098    DISPLAY `<TR BGCOLOR="lightblue">`
000099    DISPLAY `<TD>TCPIP-HOSTENT-ADDRESS(`  &  counter  &  `): </TD>`
000100    DISPLAY `<TD>`  &  TCPIP-HOSTENT-ADDRESS(counter)  &  ` </TD>`
000101    DISPLAY `</TR>`
000102  END-PERFORM.

   Why not telling the compiler, or the run-time system that I want to  
have a HTML document, which would then result in prefixing every  
DETAIL group with a '<LI>', and generating an HTML table with  
'<TABLE>', '<TR></TR>', and <TD></TD>' tags, when the COBOL definition  
is subject to an OCCURS clause? Why should the source program care  
about such details as those tags? Leave that to the compiler and run- 
time system, and avoid errors.

   And why not leaving it to the compiler and run-time sytem to insert  
unit-separators between items and line-separators (LF, CR, or LF/CR)  
when I want to create a CSV file?

   This is the direction which I would like the next standad to  
enhance the REPORT WRITER. I have not yet had a look into the  
standardizing work on XML, though.

HB> Often I create files that are read in by a PC spreadsheet or
HB> database.    The users have the expertise to do whatever they want
HB> from that stage.

   Sure, so let's make it easier to create such an output using COBOL.


Yours,
Lï¿½ko Willms                                     http://www.willms-edv.de
/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --

Seine Bï¿½cher waren alle sehr nett, sie hatten auch sonst wenig zu tun. -G.C.Lichtenberg
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Report enhancements

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms)
- **Date:** 2004-10-22T14:54:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9JLTANBPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<41659C51.30908@optonline.net> <9J4yzBgeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <cl5r85$ate$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
.    On  20.10.04
  wrote  howard@brazee.net (Howard Brazee)
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
     in  cl5r85$ate$1@peabody.colorado.edu
  about  Report enhancements


HB> But what we want (in Mainframe land) nowadays is to have a mainframe
HB> CoBOL program to create a report which the users can view on their PC
HB> and then decide to print on their printers set up the way they want,
HB> and in the format they want.    PC CoBOL has this same requirement -
HB> unless each computer has its separately compiled copy of the main
HB> program REPORT WRITER doesn't do enough.
HB>
HB> Often I create files that are read in by a PC spreadsheet or
HB> database.    The users have the expertise to do whatever they want
HB> from that stage.
HB>
HB> When one office wants to change its report, the program doesn't have
HB> to be changed and tested by all offices.

   I would distinguish two types of printouts:

1) Those which actually represent a transaction and are bound to a  
specific form, like invoices, bank forms, payment slips, tax forms,  
social security forms, and things like that. I would not want some  
department to tamper with the form of those documents.


2) Informational reports about, e.g. merchandise stock levels, sales  
figurs per period and region, price lists, and so on. These were at  
least in part necessary in earlier times when the whole data  
processing was done by batch runs. Nowadays I would recommend not to  
produce printed reports, but to look up those data online in the  
central database, and to print it out on one's own account and format  
as you please, and only if deemed necessary.

   Even in the old days, those reports were not always really needed.  
There are enough stories where the DP department did not produce one  
of those reports, and nobody noticed it...


   And coming back to the means that the programming language COBOL  
provices, I think that even a report presented as an HTML page could  
be produced by the REPORT WRITER feature, to which I would recommend  
an enhancement which inserts all the necessary HTML tags to structure  
the document, which can then be visualized using some CSS style sheet,  
just as you explained. And then printed, or not...


Yours,
Lï¿½ko Willms                                     http://www.willms-edv.de
/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --

Da liegen nun die Kartoffeln und schlafen ihrer Auferstehung entgegen. -G.C.Lichtenberg
```
